0064471837|5.0|Sabriel drew me in from the first page. Years later this book still hasn't let me out. Before you start to think that this is just another derivative Undead Teen Fantasy, I should point out that Sabriel predates the books of the zombie/vampire popularity wave by a decade. B00CM9JKQM|3.0|Throughout rock history there have been many groups who have undergone personnel changes. While Queensryche has had a revolving door in their lead guitar department, this effort marks the first time they have chosen to utilize a new vocalist. Previous groups who have successfully been able to amend the voice of their band include Van Halen and Black Sabbath. However, in such cases it should be noted that those singers had highly successful careers before joining these groups and brought a large fan base with them when they joined (Sammy Hagar solo and with Montrose, Ronnie James Dio with Rainbow). Groups such as Journey and Styx have not experienced such great success when replacing their singers and undergo such stigmas as being labeled a karaoke band. New Queensryche singer, Todd La Torre brings with him no measurable fan base and now faces such a dilemma.<br />Their first effort on the self-titled disc begins with the instrumental track X2, which is far less a musical endeavor and more reminiscent of a soundtrack that you would expect to hear in a movie or video game. Although this is not atypical for Queensryche, knowing fan expectations it comes off as being quite contrived. \\"Where Dreams go to Die\\" marks the single contribution from added guitarist Parker Lundgren on this release and marks the debut of new vocalist La Torre. While the song starts out as an aggressive metal song, it quickly transforms itself with a smoky lounge type verse and an over the top clich√© chorus with the singer promising the listener \\"I will take you there.\\"<br />If there is any track on this disc that is remotely reminiscent of the early Queensryche sound it is found in the track \\"Spore.\\" Rockenfield in particular shines on this endeavor. Subsequent offerings such as \\"In This Light\\" and \\"Vindication\\" would lend themselves better to being labeled as Iron Maiden songs then to suggest that it sounds like classic Queensryche. Redemption, although a very fine track, has the vocals sounding quite processed throughout the choruses (autotuned?) and La Torre seems to be straining his upper register in an effort to make it work. \\"Midnight Lullaby\\" is another brief interlude used as an introduction to \\"A World Without\\" which is not only unnecessary, but continues to show a calculated move on the part of the group (or the producer) who are trying to make it sound like former Queensryche releases in their attempt to appease their die-hard fan base. \\"Don't Look Back\\" and \\"Fallout\\" are two rocking songs that will surely get some fans pumping their fists in the air but at times comes off as sounding a little too commercialized for hard-core metal fans looking to find that vintage Queensryche sound.  The offering of newly recorded originals concludes with \\"Open Road,\\" in typical fashion of the way that all Queensryche albums end - a slow song.<br />While this \\"new Queensryche\\" album is a very solid effort, and I find it quite enjoyable, it does not sound like any Queensryche we've ever heard before. Michael Wilton will always sound like Michael Wilton whether or not he plays on a Queensryche album or some other album.  Jackson, who I find as being one of the top bass players of the whole metal genre will always shine but continue to be under rated and underestimated. Of course Rockenfield is always a good drummer that can lay down a solid effort; however being a rhythmical instrument and not a musical instrument, it does not impact the overall sound/vibe of the music. That being said, because one individual or a group of individuals playing is recognizable does not make it any more Queensryche sounding then something as simple as hearing the voice from their former singer. Had the disc not concluded with a live recording of three classic Queensryche songs, which is a good thing, one might not know who they were supposed to be listening to.<br />All in all it is a very good album; just not a Queensryche Album. Under another moniker I could have given it four stars. Under the brand of Queensryche and comparing it to their earliest works, I have to stretch in order to give it three. Nothing on this disc sounds anything like: the EP, The Warning , or Rage For Order (Queensryche in general).

140006922X|1.0|Listened to the book, the comments below explain the book better than I have heard anywhere else.    Zealot recently received a publicity boost from an awkward interview, which Joe Carter analyzes here. (But amidst the hub-bub about the interview, which focused on why a Muslim would write a book on Jesus, Joe wonders why people aren't snickering at Dr. Aslan himself, who seems to be more of an amateur historian--with no teaching experience on, or peer-reviewed academic articles about, historical Jesus research--who is implying that he is an expert in the field. As Alan Jacobs notes, \\"Reza Aslan's book is an educated amateur's summary and synthesis of a particularly skeptical but quite long-established line of New Testament scholarship, presented to us as simple fact. If you like that kind of thing, Zealot will be the kind of thing you like.\\")    Ross Douthat has a good post on the book, and Mike Bird will be reviewing the book for TGC.    The Good Book Company Blog also has a review by Gary Manning Jr., associate professor of New Testament at Biola University's Talbot School of Theology.    Here's the conclusion:      Finally, despite his generally good understanding of the field, Aslan makes a number of significant errors. I took pages of notes just on historical and linguistic errors in Zealot. Here are only a few examples of significant scholarly errors:      Use of Greek definitions not found in any standard Greek lexicon;    Using the wrong Greek lexicon for the New Testament;    Incorrect definition of the targumim;    Unawareness of the evidence for high literacy in ancient Israel;    Unawareness of literary approaches to the gospels;    Claims that violence against foreigners was the only faithful Jewish response;    Claims that Pilate crucified \\"thousands upon thousands\\" without trial;    Very late, unlikely dates for the writing of the four gospels;    Claims that ancient people did not understand the concept of history;    Claims that Luke was knowingly writing fiction, not history;    Claims that Mark does not describe Jesus' resurrection;    And on and on.      In many cases, I had to come to the conclusion that Aslan was just not familiar enough with modern scholarship related to the New Testament.      There are numerous other problems with Zealot, too numerous to address in an already-too-long blog post. Aslan repeatedly presents highly unlikely interpretations of passages in the New Testament, makes little effort to defend those interpretations, then moves on as if he has made his case. Suffice to say this, as others have said before: there is something a little bizarre about using our only historical documents about Jesus (the New Testament) to come to conclusions quite in opposition to those documents. There is a good reason to believe that Jesus claimed to be a divine king and savior who would die and rise again, and would one day return to judge the world: All four gospels, and indeed the entire New Testament, make this claim. You can deny that this claim is true, but it is scholarly folly to deny that Jesus and the early Christians believed it.    The whole post is here:  [...]

1490313370|5.0|I had never heard of James Altucher before I was given this book.  It came as a birthday present, alongside what ended up feeling like a pile of self-help books.  I know that the gift giver did not intend my birthday haul to resemble the self-help aisle of a bookstore (plus flip flops), but it certainly felt that way.  Oh right, there was a bottle of tequila too!  So, forgive me if it took me a while to bother even looking at this book, but here is what I have to say about it, now that I've read it:<br /><br />I liked it.  There are plenty of life lessons in this book, and sure, the requisite amount of cheese.  Although, I suppose many would simply see that bit as \\"accessible\\"?  Regardless, it is certainly written with a level of informality that will offend some and attract others.  I don't agree with everything that Altchur says, but the thing that I really liked about this book is that it feels like a well articulated hard copy of the life-lessons that I have already learned but can't manage to hold on to; like an outline of things I keep trying to remember all at once and keep forgetting.  We all have our lifelong battles.  My own tend to focus on maintaining momentum.  Someone once said that \\"life is like riding a bicycle. It's all about balance and forward momentum.\\"  Reading this book feels a little like my dad holding my bicycle handlebars when I was just starting to internalize what it felt like to hit the groove of riding a two wheeler.<br /><br />Did I mention I'm 32?<br /><br />The other thing that I liked about this book is that it totally validated some of my own beliefs.  I don't work in a bank, ergo why do I have to wake up at 7am if that just doesn't work for me?  People LOVE to judge, and haters are going to hate.  Having someone tell you that it is okay to make your own rules, while at the same time reminding you that if you're going to make your own rules then you have to ACTUALLY follow through and make them, is a good combination.<br /><br />You can't expect to get all of life's answers out of a book.  You can't expect anyone else to live your life for you.  I think that people who don't like this book are looking for the answers to their problems in a book.  This book isn't the answer to your problem, but it just might help you stop long enough to see a doorway that you would have otherwise missed.<br /><br />Another thing---Altucher pronounces the American Dream dead.  Time of Death: 2008.  Personally, I understand the point that he is trying to make.  To a large degree, I agree with him, but anyone who still lives, works, or aspires to be a part of \\"corporate America\\" is not going to like the fact that he actually said it in print.  Not to mention to the fact that there are certain industries that aren't dead yet (although even industries like law and medicine are changing very fast). Now---Mr. Altucher, please correct me if I am wrong----I think what he is trying to say is that there is no longer any guaranteed path to earning a living.  You can still go to Law school and become a lawyer.  You can still got to Medical school and become a doctor.  You can still get an MBA---end of sentence (not going to even try to add anything to that one).  In the past, if you did any one of those three things, you were pretty much guaranteed a financial future.  The time when a J.D., M.D., or M.B.A. equaled lifelong income, is over.<br /><br />The point is that that doesn't have to be a BAD thing.  Sure, it's scary, but it is also a future ripe with opportunity.  If you can't get behind the statement I just made, then don't bother reading the book.  if you can agree with even part of it, then read it.<br /><br />Having said that, I think the way that this issue is approached in the book doesn't quite translate. If you want to have a conversation about whether or not the American Dream is in fact dead, then have one, but this book isn't really about whether or not it's true.  This book is built on the premise that the paradigm has already shifted.  The state of the U.S. job market is such that you could write an entire encyclopedia on the dynamics of employment.  That's not the point. This book takes it as a given that if you're reading it, you probably already have a sense that things are no longer the way they once were.  If you are already looking for a way around the establishment, then no problem; Altucher gives you tools to try and forge a new pathway.  If you are part of the establishment and perfectly content to stay there, then you're not going to like the fact that Altucher just said \\"what you think you have no longer exists.\\"<br /><br />There is a line in the book where Altchur says you have two choices: be a temp or be an entrepreneur.  I think the \\"temp\\" part of this sentence is broader than temp agencies, or \\"dead-end\\" jobs that pay the rent.  I think Mr. Altucher probably meant that you have to get used to the fact that your \\"job\\" even your \\"career\\" is temporary---again, please correct me, If I am wrong.<br /><br />If you can't accept that the ground has already shifted beneath our feet, then you're going to have a problem with this book.  If you pick up this book because you recognize that the ground has already shifted?  Then \\"Choose Yourself\\" is a great little toolbox, full of ways to build or rebuild part or all of your life.<br /><br />FInally, I would like to add one more thing.  I would have like to given this book 4.5 stars, but that's not an option. 4.5 because (1) I'm not in love with the cover and (2) there is certainly a little cheesiness/informality within, and (3) I don't like the title.  Now, if you've read the book, hopefully you'll get the... joke? irony? self-deprecation? symmetry?  I don't know. I just know that I liked the content a whole lot---not quite enough to give it my all-time high rating, but high enough that I'll give it 5 stars instead of 4.  Besides, 4.5 should be rounded up to 5 anyway.

B008220DIC|4.0|\\"Comparisons are not invariably odious, but they are often misleading,\\" Orson Welles once wrote, in reference to the long-running debate over whether or not the many parallels in his film \\"Citizen Kane\\" to the real life story of William Randolph Hearst and the rise of his powerful publishing empire were purely coincidental. It is quite possible that current and future generations of critics and audiences will engage in similar debate regarding the parallels in writer-director Paul Thomas Anderson's latest film, \\"The Master to the real life story of L. Ron Hubbard and the founding of his Church of Scientology.<br /><br />Despite a number of \\"coincidences\\", the answer to the most obvious question is, \\"no\\". This is neither a hagiography nor a smack down of any specific doyen or belief system (thinly disguised or otherwise). Anyone who would pigeonhole the film with such a shallow reading likely has not seen it (or is perhaps unfamiliar with certain prevalent themes running through all of PTA's previous films). What he has crafted is a thought-provoking and startlingly original examination of why human beings in general are so prone to kowtow to a burning bush, or an emperor with no clothes.  Is it a spiritual need? Is it an emotional need? Or...is it purely a lizard brain response, embedded in our DNA?<br /><br />As Inspector Clouseau once ruminated, \\"Well you know, there are leaders...and there are followers.\\" At its most rudimentary level, \\"The Master\\" is a two-character study about a leader and a follower (and metaphorically, all leaders and followers. Anderson frames his narrative using the zeitgeist of America's existential post-WW2 malaise, in the person of ex-sailor Freddie Quell (Joaquin Phoenix). Socially withdrawn, prone to dipsomania, odd sexual compulsions and unpredictable fits of rage, Freddie's transition back to civilian life has not been a smooth one. In fact, the character of Freddy strongly recalls the archetypal \\"disillusioned vet\\" protagonist who pops up time and again throughout the classic film noir cycle of the 1940s and 1950s.<br /><br />Freddie's laundry list of personality disorders has not endeared him to the 5 o'clock world; he drifts from job to job. Headed for a meltdown, Freddy skulks in the shadows of a San Francisco marina, where he crashes a shipboard wedding party, hoping to stow away. It turns out that the ship is captained by the father of the bride, Lancaster Dodd (Philip Seymour Hoffman). Dodd is a self-described writer/doctor/nuclear physicist/ philosopher and \\"...a hopelessly inquisitive man.\\"  He is also a burgeoning cult leader; the boat is chock-a-block with devotees in thrall with Dodd and his philosophy, referred to as \\"The Cause\\".<br /><br />Initially, the paranoid Dodd admonishes his uninvited guest, suspecting him to be some manner of government spook assigned to infiltrate his organization; but instead of giving him the heave-ho, \\"something\\" compels him to do a sudden 180 and invite the twitchy and troubled Freddie along for an imminent (Homeric?) ocean voyage with his family and followers to New York. And so begins the life-altering relationship between the two men, which vacillates tenuously between master/servant, mentor/apprentice, and father/son (the latter recalling Philip Baker Hall and John C. Reilly in \\"Hard Eight\\", Burt Reynolds and Mark Wahlberg in \\"Boogie Nights\\", Tom Cruise and Jason Robards in \\"Magnolia\\", and Daniel Day-Lewis and Dillon Freasier/Paul Dano in \\"There Will Be Blood\\").<br /><br />Not to denigrate Hoffman, who is mesmerizing as always; nor fine supporting performances from the likes of Amy Adams (as Dodd's subtly controlling wife, who plays a sort of shrewd Livia to his mercurial Augustus) or Laura Dern,but Phoenix achieves an Oscar-worthy transformation. I don't know if this was by accident or by design, but I swear he is channeling Montgomery Clift, not only replicating his acting tics and vocal inflection, but his physicality (right down to the hunched shoulders and sunken chest-it is downright eerie).<br /><br />The film is beautifully shot in 65mm by DP Mihai Malainare, Jr., and nicely scored by Jonny Greenwood. Those with short attention spans are warned: This film demands your full attention (and begs repeated viewings). It's exhilarating, audacious, and while at times a bit baffling, it is never dull.

B0000CDLA7|4.0|Inspired by a favorable reaction to some of Dream Theater's heaviest songs (played while the band was on tour), \\"Train Of Thought,\\" the 2003 opus from the New York-based, but world-renowned and massively influential progressive stalwarts finds the band opting for a more-or-less stripped down, and more accessible and straightforwardly heavy sound. And hate me for saying this if you must, but James LaBrie sounds like he listened to a lot of modern rock radio before recording his vocal parts for this album, as they sound like they are influenced by groups not unlike A.F.I., Korn, and Alice In Chains. This gives the musical arrangements an undeniable nu-ish-flavored quality, and one that is likely to ruffle the feathers of many-a-fan.<br /><br />But with that said, most of the usual D.T. trademarks are still firmly in place on \\"T.O.T.,\\" including (but not limited to) both heavy and melodic guitar riffs, technically-proficient leads, air-guitar-worthy solos from legendary seven-string ace John Petrucci, and really complex and dexterous drumming from skinsman Mike Portnoy. In addition, the song structuring might be more traditional and simplistic this time around, but there are frequent flights into proggy territory, with extended and drawn out instrumental sections that yield the songs as very unique, and meticulously-crafted. And plus, \\"Train\\" is another extremely epic affair, clocking in at a whopping sixty-nine-and-a-half minutes in length.<br /><br />\\"As I Am\\" opens the album with a wall of ominous guitar feedback that will make the hair on the back of your neck stand straight up before kicking in with an aggressive, heavy, chugging main riff that evokes Metallica's \\"Enter Sandman.\\" This is then iced by an accomplished and fairly warm-sounding clean vocal line, some choral backing vocals, and a strong, pronounced, depressive bass line that grumbles up from beneath the mix. And a terrific, ripping, and technically-impressive guitar solo caps the whole thing off. The Iron Maiden-influenced \\"This Dying Soul,\\" then, is centered around a muscular, lumbering, and positively doomy-sounding guitar line which is peppered by some hefty double bass propulsion, well-placed pinch harmonics, pronounced, moaning bass lines, and three excellent and incredibly wailing guitar solos. A classical music-derived piano line is also sprinkled into the mix, here, as are some soaring, old-school metal-approved vocals that sound along the same line as Ozzy Osbourne-meets-Chris Cornell. And there is a fine bit of epic, tasty, fiery, and instantly memorable guitar soloing to end the song, too.<br /><br />The dreary, dreamy melodic intro graced by guitar feedback, droning, bubbling bass notes, and colorful acoustic strumming, establish a very soothing and atmospheric vibe in the intro to \\"Endless Sacrifice.\\" And the song maintains this heavily progressive feel throughout its verses -- right up until the point when it reaches its choruses, which are suddenly much louder and heavier, thus evoking grunge and modern-day hard rock. But this song, while distinctly modern-sounding, is well over eleven minutes long (it is a whopping eleven minutes and twenty seconds long, to be exact). And furthermore, it is un-modern in that it sneaks in some new-wavy-sounding keyboards, a Gojira/Mastodon-esque riff, and a terrifically epic guitar solo section (where two fairly melodic, slow, and involved solos are sandwiched in between two longer and more blazing and shred-oriented solos.) The drumming is also of note in this song because it grows gradually heavier and faster the more the tune plays, and climaxes with some exceptionally deft and booming drum fills.<br /><br />Next up, \\"Honor Thy Father\\" is a much more accessible and traditional slab of heavy music. It begins by blasting off with an excellent, five-second long drum solo before settling into a lurching, bullying guitar groove (that evokes Pantera), tattooed by steady, bubbling bass notes and heavy, punching riffs.The track sneaks in some ominous, spoken word samples and chaotic, shredding guitar solos, too. But no matter how heavy and hard-rockin' \\"Honor Thy Father\\" might be, it does not totally forget about Dream Theater's melodic sensibility, as it does feature a fine bit of infectious sweep picking. And this flows perfectly into \\"Vacant,\\" a somber piano and violin-based ballad that clocks in at less than three minutes in length, making it really more of an interlude than anything else.<br /><br />And it flows right into the subsequent \\"Stream Of Consciousness,\\" which finds a nice little introductory guitar melody being smashed to bits by thunderous power chords. This entirely instrumental epic (the longest instrumental song in Dream Theater's whole discography!) is a quirky and at times even mildly funky piece, and one that comes with plenty of noodling guitar leads, sweep-picked chord progressions, and unorthodox soloing that give the tune a strong Eighties glam metal feel. And rounding out the album is \\"In The Name Of God,\\" an epically sprawling, System Of A Down-reminiscent closer. But despite being a good song, and one that has plenty of sweeping melodies, crunchy guitar licks, and flavorful guitar melodies to go around, it ultimately comes across sounding a bit tacked-on and unnecessary. And at roughly fourteen minutes in length, it is overlong, too. As such, \\"In The Name...\\" should probably have been scrapped from the record, as it does not possess much staying power or replay value.<br /><br />That slight misstep aside, though, \\"Train Of Thought\\" is a very consistent and rewarding front-to-back album. Indeed, ignore the naysayers, because \\"T.O.T.\\" is is more than worthy of being labeled another fine and all-around extremely solid effort from D.T., a band that would probably rather die (or at least dissolve) than release anything else.The track sneaks in some ominous, spoken word samples and chaotic, shredding guitar solos, too. But no matter how heavy and hard-rockin' \\"Honor Thy Father\\" might be, it does not totally forget about Dream Theater's melodic sensibility, as it does feature a fine bit of infectious sweep picking. And this flows perfectly into \\"Vacant,\\" a somber piano and violin-based ballad that clocks in at less than three minutes in length, making it really more of an interlude than anything else.<br /><br />And it flows right into the subsequent \\"Stream Of Consciousness,\\" which finds a nice little introductory guitar melody being smashed to bits by thunderous power chords. And at roughly fourteen minutes in length, it is overlong, too. As such, \\"In The Name...\\" should probably have been scrapped from the record, as it does not possess much staying power or replay value.<br /><br />That slight misstep aside, though, \\"Train Of Thought\\" is a very consistent and rewarding front-to-back album. 0064471837|4.0|Sabriel, by Garth Nix, is a unique entry in the fantasy genre, or so it seems to me. I will admit, I'm not as well-read as I could be, but I have never read anything else like Sabriel or its sequels. Nix has created a world with some really clever, original rules.<br /><br />A quick breakdown of those rules: Sabriel takes place in two countries. One of these, Ancelstierre, is quite like our own early 20th-century Europe. The other, the Old Kingdom, is more reminiscent of medieval Europe and unlike Ancelstierre, is rife with magic. The two meet at a border demarcated by the Wall, where magic begins to grow weaker the farther one travels toward Ancelstierre until eventually it ceases to exist at all.<br /><br />Magic is divided into two types: Charter Magic, governed by \\"The Charter,\\" and Free Magic, which exists outside the Charter. Generally, those who use Free Magic are unscrupulous people or creatures. Free Magic is used by necromancers, who can travel between Life and Death and control the spirits of the Dead. Only one person has the abilities of a necromancer while also abiding by the Charter - the Abhorsen, whose duty it is to protect Life and the Living from the Dead and their vassals.<br /><br />Sabriel takes a little while to really pick up the pace. The downside of having created such robust rules for his world is that Nix must take a lot of time explaining how they work for the audience's benefit. The protagonist, the titular Sabriel, acts as a convenient audience stand-in, as she lived most of her life in Magic-less Ancelstierre and so is only cursorily familiar with how things work in the Old Kingdom.<br /><br />Once a good deal of the exposition is out of the way, however, Sabriel really takes off. The plot is engaging and well-paced, with moments of high action punctuated by creepy tension. The characters are well-developed with their own distinct personalities. They fit into some archetypes while breaking free of others. The result is that they mesh with fantasy genre enough for the reader to be comfortable but aren't the same tired faces that have been seen so many times before. The world in which they exist is similar; it feels like a perversion of a typical fantasy world, but not so twisted that I wanted to reject it outright. Rather, I was drawn in, disarmed by the differences enough that much of the book was able to take me by surprise.<br /><br />That isn't to say the book completely surprised me. I gave it four stars out of five for a reason. Some of the major secrets of the book, people's identities and such, were pretty easy for me to pick up. So easy that when the reveal rolled around, there wasn't even a feeling of smugness that I solved the puzzle early.<br /><br />Overall, Sabriel presents a unique and engaging world, a generally exciting plot, and lovely, well-developed characters, though it suffers from slow pacing early on and can be predictable at times.<br /><br />One last note: the cover art for the U.S. edition (I don't know if it's different in other places) is lovely. I worked in a children's library in high school, and I remember being quite struck by it on those occasions when I had to shelve the book.ith fantasy genre enough for the reader to be comfortable but aren't the same tired faces that have been seen so many times before. A wall separates the two sides. Sabriel is the daughter of the current Abhorsen, and when he sends a book and bells to Sabriel, she knows something's happened to her father, and she needs to go home, and possibly be the next in the long line of Abhorsens.<br /><br />The Abhorsens regulate the dead spirits and other spirits through the ringing of bells. As necromancer tools, the bells channel magical powers to call and send spirits to wherever the bell ringers choose, but they can be dangerous, because your own spirit can be sent into the underworld if you aren't careful. 0064471837|5.0|Sabriel (and Nix's other works) usually get shelved in the Young Adult section, placed next to the other books marketed towards girls. This is a mistake, as Garth Nix's book is well-written and engaging enough that you don't need to be female or teenaged to enjoy the twilight world that Sabriel, the title character and heroine, stumbles into defending. The pacing is constant and absorbing, as Nix doesn't fall into the common fantasy trap of explaining the vaaaast history and geography of his world. Instead, he does exactly what a writer should - by showing the reading important snippets here and there in the action, we learn organically about the world, the villains, and what Sabriel must do to save it.<br /><br />One word of warning: Read Sabriel, and you'll inevitably end up reading everything else Nix has written, and he will leave you wanting much, much more.
B000JMKTLO|3.0|Let me start by saying I was awestruck by Gone Girl, which was my first Flynn novel. I had read many reviews before ordering and was ready to put up with the unlikable characters, the sketchy plot, the twisted scheme. What I was not prepared for was HOW unlikable the characters actually are, pretty much all of them. I think the only character I could feel any empathy for was the father of one of the victims.<br />Anyway, as a whodunnit, Sharp Objects is very flawed, not all that interesting, not all that recommendable. However, as a study of human character, it is still worthy of reading, as there are a few gems here and there. Ms Flynn has unique and very intriguing views on feminism that, as a female myself, I find deeply interesting. B0082Z1CVQ|4.0|I finally got around to reading LITTLE WOMEN and don't quite know what to say. It follows the growing up of four young Yankee girls, showing their altered circumstances over about a decade, from marriage to other fates. What stands out for me now in 2013 is how preachy the intruding author is, quite treacly at times, with all that gushing over domestic pleasures and babies who talk baby-talk till you want to run away. It's not that I don't like domestic pleasures, but books like LITTLE WOMEN set up expectations that I believe are rarely realized in actual life, and people are better off not being misled by false expectations. 0439023513|5.0|MOCKINGJAY (Pub. 2010) by Suzanne Collins is the third book in the Hunger Games trilogy and is the most heart-wrenching of the three books.<br /><br />Unlike the first two books, which both feature Katniss fighting for her life during the Games, Mockingjay is about the actual war between the Capitol and the District rebels.  Although Katniss sees it as ultimately being another form of the Games, she takes part in fighting the Capitol, which she's resented from page one.<br /><br />I've read reviews and heard people say they didn't like the third book as well as the first two.  I felt the same way the first time I read the trilogy.  I've read it a second time now, devouring the books once again in a matter of a few days.  After the second reading, I am much more impressed by Mockingjay, and even more so by the author's incredible talent.<br /><br />What I like best about this third book, outside of the story itself, is how much it makes a person stop and really think about their actions, and the actions and true motivations of the politicians in the world, and those who go along with their plans.<br /><br />As a fellow writer, I am completely in awe of Collins deft ability to cover all the bases throughout the trilogy, while keeping me absolutely fascinated with the story.  The author uses devices we writers have at hand, but in a seamless way that is completely organic to the story.<br /><br />For example, Collins finds a way to refresh the reader on events that occurred in the first two books, (which is somewhat of a prerequisite for trilogies) in a way that flows with what is happening in the present.  In a scene that occurs on pages 74-76 (hardback version), Haymitch is asking the gathered group of victors and prep crew to give him examples of times when Katniss did something that truly moved them. He goes around the room and writes down the examples given by the individuals present.  This not only reminds us of past events, but serves to further deepen how muchwe care for Katniss.<br /><br />The first time I read Mockingjay, I found myself thinking the trilogy should have ended on a brighter note.  But after completing this second read, the ending came across differently for me. Not quite so sad, and more a feeling of it ending the way it had to. And once again, completely impressed by the author.<br /><br />I could go on and on with other examples of how impressed I am with Collins writing skills.  Suffice it to say, I think she is largely unsung by the general public because everyone focuses on the incredible story itself, which is just another indicator of how truly talented this author is.<br /><br />It's been twenty-plus years since I've read a story that had such a lingering impact, with the story still on my mind days after closing the book.  This trilogy is firmly at the top of my all-time-favorite books list, and I highly recommend it to anyone who hasn't discovered this trilogy yet.  Do yourself a favor and get all three books in hand before you start reading...otherwise you'll find yourself scrambling madly to get your hands on the next volume.

This is a well written story about strange happenings in the life of a seven year old boy and his chance meeting with 11 year old Lettie Hemstock.  The story is written in the first person, and it begins with this boy coming back home 35 years later to attend a funeral. 140006922X|5.0|The historical Jesus is a topic that I'm beginning to delve into, and Reza's book has served as an excellent primer for additional reading.<br /><br />In numerous interviews, Reza has explained that his intention is not to shake anyone's faith, or to obliterate the Christian religion. Rather, he sought to humanize the man who became myth and legend in the Gospels and letters that make up the New Testament.<br /><br />That being said, he makes a number of assertions that directly contradict the Gospels in ways that will make any Christian somewhat squeamish. But while doing so, he raises a lot of really good points.<br /><br />Throughout the book, he emphasizes one thing over and over: When you read the Gospels and the letters that make up the New Testament, it is critical that you consider the audience that it is being written for. Also, realize that the writers of the Gospels and letters had a very different concept of history and accuracy that we do today. Contradictions and in some cases complete fabrications were less important than the message itself.<br /><br />I consider myself a Christian and reading this book has actually strengthened my faith and adherence to Christ's message. I feel like I have a better handle on Jesus the man, and why his message has persevered. I highly recommend this book to anyone who is a Christian and wishes to get a better understanding of the origins of your faith.<br /><br />I would also recommend reading James Tabor's book about Paul, called [[ASIN:1439123314 Paul and Jesus: How the Apostle Transformed Christianity]]. Reza touches briefly on Paul toward the end of the book. James's book is a much more in depth account.

1451626096|1.0|This review will freely discuss a plot twist that happens in the first 100 pages of this book - a plot twist that to the best of my knowledge is not based on historical facts, nor is it addressed at the end in the author's notes. From reading other reviews, it seems the cat is out of the bag, but I will err on the side of caution and mark this review as containing spoilers.<br /><br />The White Princess is the fifth book in Philippa Gregory's Cousins' War series, each book focusing on a different female lead; this book being the POV of Elizabeth, daughter of Edward IV and Elizabeth Woodville (whose story was told in The White Queen), and wife to Henry VII. The book begins after the battle at Bosworth field, and as interpreted by Ms. Gregory has young Elizabeth pining over her lost lover, Richard (her uncle!!). Elizabeth, mom and all those younger sisters come to court with the expectation that she's going to marry Henry Tudor, but Henry and mom Margaret Beaufort don't seem to be in any hurry--->>>real spoiler>>>ebil Margaret wants to make sure Elizabeth can conceive a child before the happy nuptials and orders Henry to rape her. If Elizabeth can't conceive, he'll take choice #2, younger sister Cecily.<br /><br />\\"I'm not very fond of spoiled meats, I don't want another man's leaving. The thought of Richard the Usurper pawing you about and you fawning on him for the crown makes me quite sick.\\"<br /><br />A peach of a man is Henry, and mother Margaret is some piece of work. Elizabeth, being spineless and dull as dishwater (at least as written by Ms. Gregory), makes the best of her marriage popping out the heir and the spare and other boring stuff for 300 or so pages.<br /><br />\\"Two years pass before we conceive another child...\\"<br /><br />\\"Instead I will be the shape that my husband's mother wants: a rounded fertile pear of a woman, a vessel for Tudor seed, a pot.<br /><br />There is still the mystery of the fate of Elizabeth's younger brothers Edward and Richard, who disappeared from the Tower of London never to be seen again, and not a body to be found anywhere. Which of course leads to people pretending to be one of the lost boys (or are they pretenders?), including one Perkin Warbeck. What does a loving sister to do if she recognizes her lost brother (or does she recognize him?), and does her loyalty go with the sibling or her husband and father of her children?<br /><br />Should be interesting stuff, right? Well in this case, with this author, it's not. Boring, boring, boring. The Elizabeth that Ms. Gregory gives us is a spineless doormat who parrots everything her husband says - no original dialogue to be found coming out of her mouth. Add to that mix the author's very bad habit of clubbing the reader over the head with people's names and titles (does someone really think in their private thoughts about their half-brother as \\"Thomas Grey, Mother's boy\\"?), and of course the mind-numbing repetitiveness of her prose all makes for a very underwhelming read (did we really need to hear about The Royal House of York on every page?). Speaking of that repetition, I did get a giggle out of this quote from Margaret:<br /><br />\\"Richard put them to death,\\" she repeats, as if repetition will make it so.<br /><br />I guess if you repeat something enough times, folks will start to believe it's true like so many folks believe Ms. Gregory's version of history is the real deal. Not.<br /><br />And the big twist over the fate of the young princes in the tower and who done them in as Ms. Gregory sees it? Bah!<br /><br />Do I have anything good to say about it? The font was large with generous spacing between lines, very short chapters and at least we didn't have [[ASIN:B0054U5790 Henry VII drinking the blood of sanguine young men]].<br /><br />From the author's notes (two whole pages!):<br /><br />\\"This book does not claim to reveal the truth either: it is a fiction based on many studies of these fascinating times and gives, I hope, an intothe untold stories and the unknown characters with affection and respect.\\"<br /><br />Sorry, but Henry Tudor didn't exactly get much respect in the first part of this book :/<br /><br />My copy obtained via library loan.the untold stories and the unknown characters with affection and respect.\\"<br /><br />Sorry, but Henry Tudor didn't exactly get much respect in the first part of this book :/<br /><br />My copy obtained via library loan.

B002QGUFWE|5.0|Suppose I'll start this review off the same way as the last one. I've been a fan of Lady Gaga ever since Poker Face came out, and had a huge crush on her ever since. Though this is technically an EP as it only contains 8 songs, most fans including myself consider it her second album. As described, this also comes with a second disc containing every song from The Fame, which was what this album was originally meant to be, a \\"DLC addon\\" of sorts to her first album. Here, I'll review each of the 8 songs individually as I did for The Fame.<br /><br />Bad Romance: This was the lead single, released to promote this album a few months before it came out. It's very fast paced, and lyrically it's about sharing even the darkest facets of love. It's best summed up by a quote from her that states, \\"I want the deepest, darkest, sickest parts of you that you are afraid to share with anyone because I love you that much.\\" It does a great job as a lead single and reminds me of my days of playing Halo 3: ODST, as both were released the same month.<br /><br />Alejandro: In my opinion, this is the album's weakest point, but it's far from bad. It has a very dance-able beat that starts off slow and picks up during the chorus. Supposedly the lyrics reference gays in the military, though I wasn't able to tell right off. This song sorta grew on me after a while, and hearing her sing it live at the Born This Way Ball sounded loads better than the studio version. It was the album's third and final single.<br /><br />Monster: The album's title track, and my favorite song out of the eight. The beat is fantastic, and combined with the vocals, it makes for an unforgettable earworm. The lyrics describe the desire to get out of a bad or abusive relationship versus the inability to do so as the person takes over your life. It's one of those \\"club songs\\" that still has meaning to it.<br /><br />Speechless: Easily one of my favorites on the album. Another rock/piano ballad that feels like something of a spiritual successor to The Fame's \\"Brown Eyes\\". Get ready to go on a feels trip, because this songs lyrics are about Lady Gaga pleading with her ill father to undergo life-saving open heart surgery. Needless to say, it's some moving stuff.<br /><br />Dance in the Dark: As the title might suggest, it has a very dark, ethereal feel to it. This song is about the realization of one's insecurities, as well as overcoming them. It references many famous people who died tragically. Though there is no official music video, the fan-made one is definitely something I recommend checking out.<br /><br />Telephone: My favorite out of the three singles from this album, and this one had the most hype built up around it, as it features Beyonce, who I'm also a fan of. This song and its accompanying music video serve as a sequel to \\"Paparazzi\\", with the video taking place where that one last left off. The video had a ton of production put into it and contains a ton of reference to Quentin Tarantino movies. It's a rather humorous plot, with Beyonce breaking Gaga out of jail and killing everyone there. I definitely recommend watching it.<br /><br />So Happy I Could Die: Possibly the \\"sexiest\\" song on the album, with a bouncy, dark beat and plenty of references to...well, y'know...jerkin'. It's about using things such as alcohol as an escape to a happier place during tough times, or just to escape reality itself. It's a rather depressing concept that makes for a song with deep meaning to it. Another high point to the album.<br /><br />Teeth: Ending things off is a song with a gothic, almost horror feeling to it. Seriously, the first thing the beat brings to my mind is the \\"Hang Castle\\" stage from Sonic Heroes. Lyrically, it's about the idea that there's nothing wrong with telling the truth, she wants you to show her all you've got. It has a bit of an old-school feel to it, and it's very enjoyable.<br /><br />In conclusion, this is yet another incredibly solid album from my favorite pop artist, and much like The Fame, it does a great job at standing out from most mainstream radio pop. If I had to complain about one thing, it'd be the choice of what songs to release as singles, as the best ones on the album are in fact the non-singles IMO. Either way, I highly suggest The Fame Monster if you like Lady Gaga's other two albums.p artist, and much like The Fame, it does a great job at standing out from most mainstream radio pop. When I discovered it was part of a trilogy I was thrilled.<br /><br />This is the first book in the series, focused on a world split in half, on one side of the wall is a relatively normal early-modern nation, on the other side a kingdom of magic. In this novel we follow the titular character as she walks from one world to another. 0451209338|4.0|This is the first volume of Silva's popular Gideon Allon spy novels and its an excellent thriller. Allon is an art restorer who is also an Israeli spy and assassin. He's a moody character with good reason--his wife and child were murdered as a result of his spying activities. Silva plants Allon somewhere between the action spies like Matt Helm and James Bond and the ponderous, little action spies like those in John Le Carre's novels. The result is a near perfect blend of action and suspense along with thoughtful observations on international politics.<br /><br />For my money, the most interesting character in the novel is the female protagonist--the beautiful French model who also helps out as an Israeli spy. When she appears in the  story, things really began to take off. Silva provides lots of plot twists, exotic settings and plenty of suspense and I can't wait to read the next installment in the series. A really fun read.

With his views of the dour headmaster forever altered by profound revelations, he sits for a while, overwhelmed, but certain of the course of action he must take.<br /> Soon he rises, and as he exits Hogwarts for what may be the last time, he comforts a sorrowing Ron and Hermione, pleading with them to destroy the last horcrux.<br /> At the entrance to the forest, Harry has his Gethsemene moment. His golden snitch opens to reveal a Resurrection stone, enabling him to see and speak with a few of his beloved departed, drawing strength from their words before he goes on.<br /> As the Dark Lord and Death Eaters wait, with the captured Hagrid tied to a tree, Harry calmly approaches....<br /> While all seems at its darkest at this point, and those who fought to protect Harry fear all will end in heartbreak, viewers will see how Rowling's work will end with life-affirming triumph.<br /> We will watch a young boy(Arthur Bowen) whose initials interestingly enough, form the word, \\"ASP\\", follow in the footsteps of his forbears, joining his older brother (William Dunn), and cousin(Helena Barlow) on a rare and unusual journey, with a sister( Daphne De Bestingui) and another cousin (Ryan Turner )waiting in the wings.<br /> And so the saga ends, and yet begins anew. Assuming she is still at her post as Headmistress, we wish McGonagall well in dealing with  another generation of the Potter-Granger-Weasley combination, and know, that with them around, life at Hogwarts will never be dull.

1400067820|3.0|After hearing some of the interviews of Nassim Taleb during his book tour, I was totally intrigued by the topic and arguments of this book.  Seriously, he's on to something quite profound.<br /><br />As a published author myself, I know how hard it is to get your mind around big non-fiction topics.  Unfortunately, Taleb has tried to cover too much here.  The book is simultaneously too long (and disorganized) to be a good read and too short (with critical parts of the argument edited out) to be a magnum opus.  I consider myself pretty good at absorbing complex economic, business, and math topics, but after finishing this book too little of the good stuff \\"stuck.\\"<br /><br />This is a pity, as Taleb's arguments are not just well reasoned, they are wise and meaningful.  It is possible that if you had read and fully absorbed The Black Swan and Fooled by Randomness, this book would be easy to understand and integrate.  I'll try that in a few weeks...but in the mean time, the novice reader may find the style and construction of this book to be somewhat irritating.  Still worth reading, but not a truly memorable book.
